5 min
The Benefit of Mentioning 'Khalq': From Creation to Creator
Explains that 'khalq' in the verse refers to the act of creation, which points to the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mentioning 'khalq' in the verse establishes a logical chain from the created world to the Creator, through the act of creation.

Core Idea
The world indicates God's action, and God's action indicates God.

This passage addresses a key point in Islamic theology: how do we infer the existence of God from the world? It clarifies that the world itself does not directly prove God's essence, but rather the act of creation within it does. This distinction is crucial for understanding the argument from design.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical progression.
The passage begins by quoting an objection, then responds.
2
Identify the logical chain
Find the three elements: visible effect (maf'ul), action (fi'l), and agent (fa'il). Notice how each leads to the next.
The chain is: effect → action → agent.
3
Understand the distinction between effect of action vs. essence
The author says the world is an effect of God's action, not of His essence. Why does this matter? This prevents direct comparison of creation to God.
4
Grasp the benefit of mentioning 'khalq'
The word 'khalq' highlights the act of creation as the intermediate that points to the Creator. Without it, the argument would be incomplete.
The verse mentions creation to make this logical step explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قوله وإنما يستدل على الصانع بمفعولاته لا بصفاته التي مرئية لنا. فنقول: لما كان المفعول المرئي دالاً على أن فاعلا فعله كان دالاً على فعله فيصير فعله معلوما بدلالة مفعوله ثم فعله يدل على الفاعل، إذ لا فعل يتصور بدون الفاعل فكانت دلالة المصنوع المخلوق على صنع الصانع، إذ هذا دلالة الأثر على المؤثر، والعالم أثر فعله لا أثر ذاته، ثم الفعل المدلول عليه يدل على الفاعل فكانت الدلالة على الذات في خلق السماء لا في نفس السماء، بل دلالة نفس السماء على الفعل على ما قررت فكانت فائدة ذكر الخلق هذا، والله أعلم.
As for his saying: 'One only infers the Maker [al-Sani'] from His effects [maf'ulat], not from His attributes that are visible to us.' We say: Since the visible effect indicates that an agent performed it, it indicates his action, so his action becomes known by the indication of his effect. Then his action indicates the agent, for no action can be conceived without the agent. So the indication of the made created thing [al-masnu' al-makhluq] is upon the making of the Maker, for this is the indication of the effect upon the cause [al-athar 'ala al-mu'aththir]. And the world is the effect of His action, not the effect of His essence. Then the action that is indicated indicates the agent. So the indication upon the essence is in the creation of the heaven, not in the heaven itself. Rather, the indication of the heaven itself is upon the action, as I have established. So the benefit of mentioning 'khalq' (creation) is this. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage clarifies the benefit of mentioning 'khalq' (creation) in the verse. The author addresses an objection: one can infer the Maker only from His effects (maf'ulat), not from His visible attributes. He explains that the visible effect (the created entity) indicates that an agent performed it, thereby indicating the agent's action. This action, once known, indicates the agent, because an action cannot exist without an agent. Therefore, the created thing (al-masnu') points to the act of making (al-san'), which in turn points to the Maker. The world ('alam) is an effect of God's action, not a direct effect of His essence. The indication of the essence of God is found in the act of creation itself (khalq al-sama'), not in the heaven itself (nafs al-sama'). Rather, the heaven itself indicates the action of creation, which then indicates the Creator. Hence, the word 'khalq' is mentioned to highlight the act of creation as the intermediate proof, and this is the benefit.

Key Terms

مفعول (maf'ul)
أثر (athar)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
فعل (f'l)
خلق (khalq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the created world directly indicates God's essence.
Correction: The author clarifies that the world indicates God's action, and only through that action does it indicate His essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the visible effect (maf'ul) indicate?
A) Directly the essence of the Maker.
B) First the action of the agent, then the action indicates the agent.
C) Only that the effect exists, not the agent.
D) The attributes of the Maker that are visible.

Key Takeaway
The benefit of mentioning 'khalq' is to show that the act of creation, not the mere existence of the world, is what leads us to the Creator.

?
If someone were to ask you right now, 'How do you know God exists?' would your answer reflect the careful reasoning in this lesson, or would it be a vague feeling? Commit to memorizing this proof so that it becomes a sure knowledge in your heart.

Creation and the Created Thing: A Crucial Distinction
Nasafi refutes the identity of creation and created thing using analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creation (khalq) must be distinct from the created thing (makhlūq) as argued by Imam al-Nasafi, and how to avoid confusing the two.

Core Idea
Creation is an act, not a thing – the two cannot be identical.

Imam al-Nasafi begins this passage by addressing a subtle but crucial theological point: the distinction between the act of creation and the object created. Some opponents had argued that the created thing is itself creation, which would lead to confusion about God's attributes. In this lesson, we examine his initial argument and see how he uses an analogy with God's essence and attributes to clarify this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation slowly, noting the analogy with God's essence and the attribute of justice.
The author uses a parallel argument: just as God's essence is not identical to His attribute, creation is not identical to the created thing.
2
Understand the logical analogy
Nasafi argues that if the created thing were itself creation, then by analogy God's essence would be the attribute of justice—an obviously false claim.
This shows the absurdity of equating an act with its product.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does this distinction matter? It preserves the difference between God's action and His creation, and prevents pantheistic confusion.
In Islamic theology, creation is a divine act, not a property of the object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجوز تكرير الحقيقة، ولكن بهذا لا يتبين أن المخلوق في نفسه خلق، كما لا تتبين أن ذات الله تعالى في الحقيقة صفة العدل والعدالة، وهذا واضح بحمد الله تعالى. ومما يدل على بطلان هذا الكلام وكون المتمسك به متحكما، أنهم يقولون: لا فعل للعبد بطريق الحقيقة، وإنما له الاكتساب وما يضاف إلى العباد من الفعل فهو مجاز وإن كان موجودا في كلام الله تعالى وكلام الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وكلام جميع الناس بطريق التكرير قال الله تعالى: وَافْعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ وقال: فَإِن لَّمْ تَفْعَلُوا وَلَن تَفْعَلُوا وقال: بِمَا كَانُوا يَفْعَلُونَ في مواضع لا تحصى، ومع ذلك لم يخرج ذلك عن أن يكون مجازا فكذا هذا، على أن الخلق لو كان هو المخلوق للغت فائدة ذكر الخلق في قوله تعالى: إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ لأن السماء كالسماء فيصير كأنه قال إن في السماوات والأرض فيصير ذكر الخلق لغوا ضائعا.
It is permissible to repeat the real sense, but by this it is not shown that the created thing is itself creation, just as it is not shown that the essence of God Almighty is in reality the attribute of justice and equity. This is clear, praise be to God. And among what indicates the invalidity of this speech and that the one who adheres to it is arbitrary is that they say: The servant has no action in the real sense; rather he has acquisition (al-iktisāb), and what is attributed to the servants of action is metaphorical, even if it exists in the speech of God Almighty and the speech of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and the speech of all people by way of repetition. God Almighty said: 'And do good' and said: 'If you do not, and you will never do' and said: 'For what they used to do' in countless places. Yet that does not remove it from being metaphorical; so likewise this. And further, if creation (al-khalq) were the created thing (al-makhlūq), the benefit of mentioning creation in His statement: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth' would be lost, because the heaven is like the heaven, so it would be as if He said: 'Indeed in the heavens and the earth', so mentioning creation would become vain and wasted.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that human actions are not real but merely metaphorical, and who equate creation with the created thing. The author begins by stating that even if repeating a term in its real sense is permissible, it does not prove that the created thing is itself creation, just as it does not prove that God's essence is the attribute of justice. Then he points out the arbitrariness of the opponents' claim: they say actions attributed to servants are metaphorical, even though the Quran and Hadith use them literally. He cites Quranic verses commanding action and describing people's actions—'وافعلوا الخير', 'فإن لم تفعلوا ولن تفعلوا', 'بما كانوا يفعلون'—arguing that their existence in revelation does not make them metaphorical. The core argument follows: if creation (khalq) were identical to the created thing (makhlūq), then mentioning 'خلق' in the verse 'إن في خلق السماوات والأرض' would be redundant, because one could simply say 'إن في السماوات والأرض'. This shows that creation is a distinct concept from the created thing. The author concludes that the mention of 'خلق' has a specific benefit, indicating the act of creation, not the created objects themselves.

Key Terms
الحقيقة
المجاز
الخلق
المخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the analogy with God's essence and attribute show?
A) God's essence is the same as His attribute.
B) The created thing is not itself creation.
C) Creation is a metaphor.
D) The created thing is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Creation (khalq) is the act, while the created thing (makhlūq) is the object; they are not the same.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence is a created thing (makhlūq), separate from God's ongoing act of creating you (khalq). Do you live as if you are sustained by a continuous act or as a self-sufficient object? What would change if you felt your dependence on that act every second?
?
If your deeds (your 'khalq') are distinct from the results (your 'makhlūq'), then your prayers, charity, and repentance are not the same as the states they produce. Are you focused on performing the act or on becoming the kind of person the act is meant to shape? Which matters more to you today?

Are Human Actions Metaphorical? A Refutation
Nasafi refutes metaphorical human actions and proves creation's distinctness using Quranic evidence and a redundancy argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the claim that human actions are merely metaphorical, and the argument that creation must be distinct from the created thing based on Quranic usage.

Core Idea
If human actions were metaphorical, the Quran would not command them literally; and if creation were the created thing, the word would be redundant.

In this lesson, we continue with Imam al-Nasafi's argument against opponents who deny real human action. They claim that actions attributed to servants are metaphorical, despite clear Quranic commands. Nasafi counters by citing several verses and then delivers a powerful argument: if creation were identical to the created thing, mentioning 'creation' in the Quran would be meaningless. This lesson unpacks both points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents say that the servant has no real action (fi'l) but only acquisition (iktisāb), and any action attributed to people is metaphorical.
This view is associated with some early theological schools.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Nasafi cites verses that literally command action: 'وافعلوا الخير', 'فإن لم تفعلوا', 'بما كانوا يفعلون'. These show that actions are real, not just metaphorical.
The repetition of such commands in the Quran is overwhelming.
3
Analyze the redundancy argument
If creation (khalq) were identical to the created thing (makhlūq), then saying 'خلق السماوات' would be like 'السماوات', making the word 'خلق' useless.
This proves that khalq and makhlūq are distinct concepts.
4
Understand the conclusion
Creation is an act of God, distinct from the objects created. Human actions are real (though acquired), not metaphorical, and the Quran treats them as such.

Source Text

The Indication of Creation: From Effect to Maker
The author responds to the claim that the Maker is known only through effects, not attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical chain from visible effects to the act of creation, and from the act to the Creator, and why the world indicates God's act rather than His essence directly.

Core Idea
The world is the trace of God's act, not His essence.

A common objection claims the Maker is only known through visible effects, not attributes. The author of the Tabsirah responds by clarifying the chain of inference: effects indicate an act, and acts require an agent. This lesson unpacks that argument, showing how creation points to the Creator through His activity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument. Notice the structure: an objection followed by the author's reply.
The passage uses technical terms like mafʿūl (effect) and fāʿil (agent).
2
Trace the logical chain
Identify the three steps: visible effect → act → agent. See how the author distinguishes between indication of the act and indication of the essence.
The key point: the world is a trace (athar) of the act, not of the essence.
3
Apply the distinction
Understand why mentioning 'creation' in the Quran is beneficial: it directs attention to the act, which then leads to the Agent. Reflect on how this refines our understanding of divine inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قوله وإنما يستدل على الصانع بمفعولاته لا بصفاته التي مرئية لنا. فنقول: لما كان المفعول المرئي دالاً على أن فاعلا فعله كان دالاً على فعله فيصير فعله معلوما بدلالة مفعوله ثم فعله يدل على الفاعل، إذ لا فعل يتصور بدون الفاعل فكانت دلالة المصنوع المخلوق على صنع الصانع، إذ هذا دلالة الأثر على المؤثر، والعالم أثر فعله لا أثر ذاته، ثم الفعل المدلول عليه يدل على الفاعل فكانت الدلالة على الذات في خلق السماء لا في نفس السماء، بل دلالة نفس السماء على الفعل على ما قررت فكانت فائدة ذكر الخلق هذا، والله أعلم.
As for his statement: 'The Maker is only inferred from His effects, not from His attributes that are visible to us.' We say: Since the visible effect indicates that an agent did it, it indicates his act, so his act becomes known through the indication of its effect. Then his act indicates the agent, for no act can be conceived without an agent. Thus the indication of the made thing (al-maṣnūʿ) and the created thing (al-makhlūq) is upon the making (ṣunʿ) of the Maker, for this is the indication of the trace (athar) upon the one who left the trace (muʾththir). The world is the trace of His act, not the trace of His essence. Then the act that is indicated points to the agent, so the indication is upon the essence in the creation of the heaven, not in the heaven itself. Rather, the indication of the heaven itself is upon the act, as I have established. So the benefit of mentioning creation is this, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objector claims that the Maker is only inferred from His effects (mafʿūlāt), not from attributes visible to us. The author responds by explaining the chain of inference: a visible effect (al-mafʿūl al-marʾī) indicates that an agent (fāʿil) performed it, so it indicates the act (fiʿl). Once the act is known, the act indicates the agent because every act requires an agent. Thus the created, made thing (al-maṣnūʿ al-makhlūq) indicates the making (ṣunʿ) of the Maker—this is the indication of the trace (athar) upon the one who left the trace (muʾththir). The world is the trace of God's act, not of His essence. Then the act itself indicates the agent, so ultimately the indication leads to the essence of God, but through the act of creation, not directly through the created thing itself. For instance, the heaven itself points to the act of creating, not directly to the Creator's essence. Therefore, mentioning 'خلق' in the Quran is beneficial because it directs attention to the act of creation, which then leads to the Creator. The author concludes by saying this is the benefit of mentioning creation in the verse.

Key Terms
المفعول
الفعل
الفاعل
الأثر
المؤثر
المصنوع
الصانع
الصنع
الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world directly indicates God's essence.
Correction: The world indicates God's act of creation, which then leads to knowledge of the Creator. The indication is through the act, not directly on the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the visible effect (المفعول المرئي) directly indicate?
A) The essence of the Maker
B) The act of the agent
C) The attributes of the Maker
D) The material of which it is made

Key Takeaway
The created world is a sign pointing to the act of creation, and through that act we come to know the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Distinguishing Act of Creation from Created Thing
The author distinguishes between the made thing and the act of making, using Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act of making (ṣunʿa) is the direct indicator of the Maker, while the made thing (maṣnūʿ) indicates indirectly, and how this distinction is essential for a meaningful inference from the universe to the Creator, as demonstrated by a Quranic proof.

Core Idea
The universe points to the Creator through the act of creation, not directly.

The author confronts a common theological claim that the world itself directly indicates the Maker. He clarifies that the direct indicator is the act of making, and the made thing indicates only indirectly. He then offers a profound Quranic argument that only makes sense if we maintain this distinction between creation (the act) and the created thing (the result).

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the opponent's statement: 'Whatever indicates the Maker is His made thing.' Notice how the author denies this and rephrases the relationship.
The opponent conflates the act with the product.
2
Grasp the three-layer indication
Identify the three elements: the Maker, the act of making (ṣunʿa), and the made thing (maṣnūʿ). Understand the chain: made thing indicates act, act indicates Maker.
This is the key conceptual move.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Read the verse 'The creation of the heavens and the earth is greater than the creation of mankind' (40:57). See how the author uses it to show that if creation and created were identical, the verse would be trivial.
4
Reflect on the analogy from the witnessed world
The author gives an example: building a tall palace is a greater act than building a small one. This supports the interpretation that the verse compares acts of creation, not objects.
This analogy strengthens the argument for God's power to resurrect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله وما دل على الصانع فهو مصنوعه قلنا: هذا ممنوع بل ما دل على الصانع فهو صنعة، وما دل على الصنع فهو المصنوع، فكانت دلالة المصنوع على الصانع بواسطة دلالته على الصنع وهكذا الجواب عن تعلقه بجميع ما تلي من الآيات على أن قوله تعالى: لَخَلْقُ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ أَكْبَرُ مِنْ خَلْقِ النَّاسِ من أدل الدلائل على بطلان مذهبه، لأن الأمر لو كان على ما زعم لكان تقدير الكلام، السماوات والأرض أكبر من الناس ولا فائدة فيه، إذ كل أحد يعرف هذا بالمشاهدة وليس مراد الباري جل وعلا من الآية هذا، بل مراده والله أعلم، أن خلقهما في تقدير عقولكم أكبر من خلق الناس، إذ في الشاهد كلما كان المفعول أكبر كان الفعل أكبر، إذ بناء قصر عال أكبر من بناء صغير، فيستدل والله أعلم بخلقه السماوات والأرض على قدرته على خلق الناس عند الإعادة، فأما لو كان الخلق والمخلوق واحدا فلا تحصل هذه الفائدة.
And his statement: 'Whatever indicates the Maker is His made thing.' We say: This is denied. Rather, what indicates the Maker is His act of making (ṣunʿa), and what indicates the act of making is the made thing (al-maṣnūʿ). Thus the indication of the made thing upon the Maker is through its indication upon the act of making. And likewise the answer to his clinging to all the verses he recited. Moreover, His statement Exalted: 'The creation of the heavens and the earth is greater than the creation of mankind' is among the strongest proofs of the invalidity of his doctrine. Because if the matter were as he claims, the estimation of the speech would be: 'The heavens and the earth are greater than people'—and there is no benefit in that, for everyone knows this by observation. And this is not the intent of the Blessed and Exalted from the verse. Rather, His intent—and God knows best—is that His creating them in the estimation of your minds is greater than creating people, because in the witnessed world, whenever the effect (mafʿūl) is greater, the act (fiʿl) is greater. For example, the building of a tall palace is greater than the building of a small one. So one infers—and God knows best—by His creation of the heavens and the earth His power to create people at the time of resurrection. Now, if creation and the created thing were one, this benefit would not be obtained.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific claim: 'Whatever indicates the Maker is His made thing (maṣnūʿ).' The author denies this, saying that what indicates the Maker is actually the act of making (ṣunʿa), while the made thing indicates the act of making. So indication of the made thing on the Maker is indirect, through the act. The author then provides a powerful Quranic proof: the verse 'لخلق السماوات والأرض أكبر من خلق الناس' (Quran 40:57). If the opponents' view that creation is identical to the created thing were true, the verse would mean 'The heavens and earth are greater than people'—a trivial fact everyone knows by observation. But this cannot be God's intended meaning. The true meaning is that the act of creating the heavens and earth is greater in your estimation than the act of creating people. This is supported by worldly analogy: a larger effect implies a greater act. Therefore, the verse proves God's power to resurrect by comparing the greater creation of the heavens and earth to the lesser creation of people. If creation (khalq) were the same as the created thing (makhlūq), this inference would be impossible because the comparison would be between objects, not acts. Thus the distinction between creation and created thing is essential for the beneficial meaning of the verse.

Key Terms
الصانع
الصنعة
المصنوع
الخلق
المخلوق
الدلالة
المفعول
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world itself directly indicates God's essence or attributes without mediation.
Correction: The world indicates the act of creation, which in turn indicates the Creator. The distinction between the act and the product is crucial.

Checkpoint
How does the author use the verse 'لخلق السماوات والأرض أكبر من خلق الناس' to refute the opponent?
A) It proves that creation is greater than the created thing.
B) It shows that if creation were identical to the created, the verse would be trivial.
C) It indicates that the heavens and earth are not created by God.
D) It demonstrates that people are greater than the heavens and earth.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between the act of creation (khalq) and the created thing (makhlūq) is essential for a meaningful inference from the universe to the Creator, and is confirmed by Quranic reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the vastness of the heavens and the earth, do you see them as pointing directly to God's existence, or do you recognize the act of creation as the necessary middle step? How does this distinction deepen your awe of His creative power, and what specific habit can you adopt to consciously acknowledge His act of creating each day?
?
The author warns against equating creation with the created thing, lest the Quranic verse lose its profound meaning. In your own life, have you ever become so focused on the 'things' God has made—your health, wealth, relationships—that you forgot the majesty of His act of bringing them into being? What would it look like for you to shift your gratitude from the gifts to the Giver's act of giving, starting today?
?
The verse proves resurrection by comparing the greater creation (cosmos) to the lesser (humans). Do you truly believe that the same God who created the immensity of space and time can raise you from the dead? If you are honest, what doubts or distractions prevent that belief from transforming how you live this week, and what one step can you take to align your actions with that certainty?

Refuting the Claim that the Evidence for the Creator is Created
Al-Nasafi refutes a claim and explains a Quranic argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical distinction between the act of making (sana) and the thing made (masnu), and how a Quranic verse refutes the idea that creation is identical to the created.

Core Idea
The evidence points to the Maker through the making, not directly from the made.

In this passage, al-Nasafi addresses a subtle objection: if the universe indicates God, is it itself created? He clarifies that the universe indicates God through its state of being made, not as a direct product. He then uses a Quranic verse to expose the absurdity of conflating creation with the created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the clear distinction between 'sana' and 'masnu'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the claim that what indicates the Maker is the made itself. Observe how al-Nasafi responds with 'this is prevented'.
3
Understand the threefold distinction
Notice the three elements: Maker (al-Sani'), making (al-Sana'), and made (al-Masnu'). The made indicates the making, which indicates the Maker.
This logical chain avoids direct identification.
4
Analyze the Quranic argument
Examine how verse 40:57 is used. If creation and created were identical, the verse would be trivial. Instead, it points to the greatness of the act of creation, inferring God's power.
The benefit is particularly for proving resurrection.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation is important: it preserves the distinction between Creator and creation and supports rational inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي يدل على الصانع صنعه]: 011 وقوله وما دل على الصانع فهو مصنوعه قلنا : هذا ممنوع بل ما دل على الصانع فهو صنعة، وما دل على الصنع فهو المصنوع ، فكانت دلالة المصنوع على الصانع بواسطة دلالته على الصنع وهكذا الجواب عن تعلقه بجميع ما تلي من الآيات على أن قوله تعالى: ﴿ لَخَلْقُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ أَكْبَرُ مِنْ خَلْقِ النَّاسِ ﴾ من أدل الدلائل على بطلان مذهبه، لأن الأمر لو كان على ما زعم لكان تقدير الكلام، السماوات والأرض أكبر من الناس ولا فائدة فيه ، إذ كل أحد يعرف هذا بالمشاهدة وليس مراد الباري جل وعلا من الآية هذا، بل مراده والله أعلم، أن خلقهما في تقدير عقولكم أكبر من خلق الناس، إذ في الشاهد كلما كان المفعول أكبر كان الفعل أكبر ، إذ بناء قصر عال أكبر من بناء صغير، فيستدل والله أعلم بخلقه السماوات والأرض على قدرته على خلق الناس عند الإعادة، فأما لو كان الخلق والمخلوق واحدا فلا تحصل هذه الفائدة.
"What indicates the Maker is His making." And his saying: "And what indicates the Maker is the made" we say: this is prevented. Rather, what indicates the Maker is the making (sana), and what indicates the making is the made (masnu). So the indication of the made upon the Maker is by means of its indication upon the making. And such is the answer about his adherence to all the verses he recited. And indeed His saying, Exalted: "The creation of the heavens and the earth is greater than the creation of mankind" (40:57) is among the most powerful evidences of the falsehood of his doctrine. Because if the matter were as he claimed, the meaning of the speech would be: the heavens and the earth are greater than mankind, and there is no benefit in that, since everyone knows this by observation. And the intention of the Creator, may He be glorified and exalted, from the verse is not this. Rather, His intention, and Allah knows best, is that His creation of them in the estimation of your intellects is greater than the creation of mankind, because in the witnessed world, whenever the effect (maf'ul) is greater, the act (fi'l) is greater—as a high palace's construction is greater than a small building's construction. So by His creation of the heavens and the earth, one infers, and Allah knows best, His power to create mankind at the resurrection. But if creation and the created were one, this benefit would not be obtained.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim that the evidence for the Creator (the universe) is itself a product of the Creator, implying that the indication is problematic. The author begins by distinguishing between the act of making (sana) and the thing made (masnu). He denies that the evidencing thing is itself the made; rather, it is the act of making that indicates the Creator. The made indicates the act, and the act indicates the maker. This preserves a logical order. Then he turns to the opponent's citation of Quranic verses, specifically 40:57. If creation is identical to the created as the opponent claims, then the verse would merely mean the heavens and earth are larger than humans, which is trivial. But the true meaning is that the act of creation is greater in terms of power and wisdom, leading to inference of God's power to resurrect.

المصنوع
الخلق
المخلوق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The evidence for the Creator (the universe) is itself a created thing, so it cannot be used as evidence.
Correction: The universe points to God through its state of being made (sana), not as a direct product. The distinction between the act of making and the thing made preserves the logical inference.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Nasafi's argument regarding the verse 'The creation of the heavens and the earth is greater than the creation of people'?
A) It proves that the heavens are larger than humans.
B) It shows that the act of creation is greater, leading to inference of God's power.
C) It establishes that creation is identical to the created.
D) It refutes the existence of the Creator.

Key Takeaway
The evidence for God is not the created things themselves but their state of being made, and the Quranic verse shows the act of creation is greater, supporting rational inference to God's power.

The Principle: God Is Not Described by the Existence of Creatures
Al-Nasafi states a principle about existents and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be described by the existence or accidentality of created things, even though He has power over them.

Core Idea
God's power does not make Him share in the creature's existence or attributes.

Al-Nasafi begins with a crucial principle: an existent thing exists by itself, and an accident is an accident by itself. God, even with absolute power, is not described by the existence that belongs to the creature's essence, nor by its accidental qualities. This sets the foundation for the argument that creation must be distinct from the created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Al-Nasafi first establishes a general rule about existents and accidents.
The rule: an existent exists by itself; an accident is accident by itself.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'الموجود لنفسه' (self-existing), 'العرض لنفسه' (self-accident), and 'الخلق' vs 'المخلوق'.
3
Understand the conclusion of this part
Al-Nasafi concludes that God is not described by the existence of the creature nor by its accidentality. Ask why that is important for the later argument.
This prepares the ground for the reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق هذا أن موجودا ما لما كان موجودا لنفسه فكذا عرض ما لما كان عرضا لنفسه، وكذا اللون والسواد لم يكن الله تعالى بثبوت القدرة له عليه موصوفا بكونه موجودا بذلك الوجود الراجع إلى ذات ذلك الموجود، ولا بكونه عرضا ولونا وسوادا بتلك المعاني الراجعة إلى ذوات تلك الأعراض، فلو كان الخلق هو عين المخلوق، لما كان الله تعالى به خالقا بل كان ما هو الخلق خالقا كما كان ما هو المكون لنفسه من الأعراض كاينًا، وما هو البقاء لنفسه من صفات الباري باقيا على ما بينا في مسألة الصفات. فإذا على هذا كل ما وجد بعد العدم فهو الخالق لنفسه لا خالق له سواه ولا موجد، وهذا هو عين التعطيل والقول بنفي الصانع، والقول بذلك موجب القول بقدم العالم، إذ ما لا موجد له وكان وجوده بنفسه كان قديما على ما قررنا فإذا لم يكن هو خالقا بنفسه فغيره الذي ليس يخلق بنفسه ولا قام به الخلق أولى أن لا يكون خالقا . والذي يحقق ما ذهبنا إليه أن لو وجد في محل وهو سواد بنفسه وهو خلق بنفسه فكان من قام به ما هو السواد قام به ما هو الخلق، ولو كان المحل هو الأسود لقيام ما هو السواد لكان ينبغي أن يكون هو الخالق لقيام ما هو الخلق به، ولو كان الباري هو الخالق لا من
And that which verifies this is that an existent insofar as it is existent by itself, and likewise an accident insofar as it is an accident by itself, and likewise color and blackness—Allah, the Most High, is not described, by virtue of the establishment of power over it, as being existent by that existence that refers back to the essence of that existent, nor as being an accident, color, blackness by those meanings that refer back to the essences of those accidents. So if creation (al-khalq) were identical to the created thing (al-makhluq), then Allah would not be a Creator by it, but rather that which is creation would be the creator, just as that which is the self-constitutive of accidents would be originating, and that which is the self-subsisting permanence of the attributes of the Creator would be permanent, as we explained in the issue of attributes. So based on this, everything that comes into existence after non-existence would be its own creator; it has no creator other than itself and no bringer into existence. And this is exactly the denial of God (al-ta'til) and the statement denying the Maker. And the statement of that necessitates the statement of the pre-eternity of the world, for that which has no bringer into existence and whose existence is by itself would be eternal, as we established. So if it is not a creator by itself, then another which neither creates by itself nor has creation subsisting in it is even more deserving of not being a creator. And that which verifies what we have gone to is that if something existed in a locus, it being black by itself and it being creation by itself, then that in which blackness subsists would be that in which creation subsists; and if the locus were the black thing due to the subsistence of blackness, it would be fitting that it be the creator due to the subsistence of creation in it; and if the Creator was the Creator not from...
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) that creation (khalq) is not identical to the created thing (makhluq). Al-Nasafi starts with a general principle: an existent (mawjūd) exists by itself, and an accident ('araḍ) is an accident by itself. God is not described by the existence that belongs to the created thing's essence, nor by the accidentality of that thing. If creation were the very same as the created thing, then God would not be the Creator via that creation; rather, the creation itself would be the creator, analogous to how an accident's self-constituent (mukawwin li-nafsihi) would be its own originator. The consequence is disastrous: everything that exists after non-existence would be its own creator, leading to ta'tīl (denial of God) and the assertion of the pre-eternity of the world (qidam al-'ālam). This is because anything with no external cause and existing by itself must be eternal. Al-Nasafi then reinforces with an illustration: if a blackness is black by itself and also creation by itself, then the locus in which blackness subsists would also be the locus in which creation subsists, and would thus be the creator. The argument concludes that if God were the Creator without creation subsisting in Him, that would be absurd.

Key Terms
الموجود لنفسه
العرض لنفسه
الخلق
المخلوق
الوجود
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because God creates, He shares in the creature's existence or attributes.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that the existence of a creature is proper to itself; God is not described by it.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi, why is God not described as 'existent' by the existence of a created thing?
A) Because God's existence is different in kind.
B) Because the existent exists by itself and its existence belongs to its own essence.
C) Because God does not have power over the creature's existence.
D) Because existence is an accident that cannot be attributed to God.

Key Takeaway
God's creative power does not make Him described by the creature's own existence or attributes; this distinction is foundational for proving that creation is not the same as the created thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself describing Allah's existence as if it were similar to the existence of a creature—just 'more powerful' or 'eternal'? This lesson declares that the very existence of a creature belongs to its own essence, and Allah is not described by it. How will you adjust your thoughts to preserve this absolute distinction? Commit to speaking of Allah's existence as utterly unique, even in your private reflections.
?
If creation were identical to the created, then creation would be creator—an absurdity. In your daily life, do you ever act as if your own actions (your 'creation') are independent of Allah, as if they have power in themselves? Where do you need to repent and reaffirm that all power belongs to Allah alone?
?
When you reflect on Allah's attributes, such as His power, are you tempted to imagine them as similar to created attributes (like human power)? This passage shows that even accidents like color are proper to creatures and not shared by Allah. If you find any anthropomorphic notion in your heart, how will you purge it and worship Allah as He truly is—beyond any comparison?

The Reductio: Consequences of Identifying Creation with the Created
Al-Nasafi delineates the logical consequences of the identity thesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disastrous theological consequences of assuming creation is identical to the created thing, including atheism and the pre-eternity of the world.

Core Idea
If creation were the same as the created, everything would be its own creator – leading to denial of God.

Having established the principle that God is not described by creature's attributes, Al-Nasafi now reveals the full absurdity of the identity thesis. If creation were identical to the created, then everything that exists after non-existence would be its own creator, making God unnecessary. This leads to the denial of God (ta'til) and the claim that the world is eternal. The passage concludes with an additional illustration involving blackness.

Guided Path
1
Read the reductio argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'فإذا على هذا' to the end. Note the chain: identity → self-creation → atheism → pre-eternity.
2
Analyze the logical steps
Identify each consequence: first, everything becomes its own creator; second, this is exactly ta'til; third, it implies qidam al-'alam.
Al-Nasafi also includes a supporting illustration with blackness.
3
Reflect on the final illustration
The last part ('والذي يحقق ما ذهبنا إليه...') gives an example: if a blackness is black by itself and also creation by itself, the locus would be the creator. Understand how this reinforces the main point.
The passage is cut off, but the idea is clear.

God is not described by the existence that belongs to the created thing's essence, nor by the accidentality of that thing. If creation were the very same as the created thing, then God would not be the Creator via that creation; rather, the creation itself would be the creator, analogous to how an accident's self-constituent (mukawwin li-nafsihi) would be its own originator. The consequence is disastrous: everything that exists after non-existence would be its own creator, leading to ta'tīl (denial of God) and the assertion of the pre-eternity of the world (qidam al-'ālam). This is because anything with no external cause and existing by itself must be eternal. Al-Nasafi then reinforces with an illustration: if a blackness is black by itself and also creation by itself, then the locus in which blackness subsists would also be the locus in which creation subsists, and would thus be the creator. The argument concludes that if God were the Creator without creation subsisting in Him, that would be absurd.

Key Terms
الخلق
المخلوق
التعطيل
نفي الصانع
قدم العالم
خالق لنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if creation were identical to the created, then God's act of creation would be unnecessary but still possible.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that identity forces self-creation, which entirely eliminates the need for God and leads to atheism.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the immediate result if creation were identical to the created thing?
A) God would still be the Creator, but in a different manner.
B) Everything would be its own creator, leading to denial of God.
C) The world would be created but without a beginning.
D) There would be no change in the doctrine of creation.

Key Takeaway
Identifying creation with the created leads to the absurd conclusion that every being is self-created, thereby denying God and implying the world's pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever act as if the things around you—your health, wealth, or abilities—are self-created rather than gifts from God? How can you remind yourself that they are created and sustained by Him alone?
?
Al-Nasafi warns that confusing the act of creation with the created thing leads to denying the Creator. Is there any area of your life where you have blurred the line between God's work and your own? What step will you take today to restore that distinction?
?
If you truly believed that every good thing in your life comes from God and is not self-generated, how would that change your attitude toward gratitude and reliance on Him? What is one concrete way you can express that reliance today?

The Ash'ari Inconsistency and Self-Existence
Author establishes self-existence principle and exposes contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari view that creation is identical to the created contradicts their own principles and violates the truth that things are self-referential in their existence.

Core Idea
If creation equals created, the created would be its own creator.

The author begins by highlighting a glaring inconsistency: the Ash'aris previously joined the Maturidis in condemning the Mu'tazila for irrational contradictions, yet they now adopt a stance that demolishes those very principles. This passage lays the groundwork by establishing that existents and accidents are what they are in themselves, and God is not described by the existence or accidenthood of things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation of inconsistency
Read the opening lines where the author notes that the Ash'aris helped refute the Mu'tazila but now contradict themselves. Notice the phrase 'هذا تناقض فاحش'.
This sets the tone for the refutation.
2
Understand the principle of self-existence
The author states that an existent is existent in itself, and an accident is an accident in itself. Grasp this as a foundational rule.
This is key to the argument.
3
Apply the principle to God's attributes
God is not described as being existent with the existence of created things, nor as being an accident or color. His power over something does not transfer those attributes to Him.
4
Consider the conditional statement
The author then presents the conditional: if creation equals created, then God would not be the creator by it; rather, creation itself would be the creator. Reflect on how this follows from the principle.
This is the pivot to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أوجد الكفر، وإن كانوا مناقضين في ذلك تاركين هذا الأصل في المتحرك والساكن وغير ذلك على ما بينا فأما الأشعرية فقد ساعدونا على تخطئه المعتزلة في تلك المسائل ونسبتهم إلى المناقضة والخروج عن المعقول ثم ها هنا أتوا بما يوجب هدم تلك الأصول وتصحيح مذاهب الخصوم، وهذا تناقض فاحش. والذي يحقق هذا أن موجودا ما لما كان موجودا لنفسه فكذا عرض ما لما كان عرضا لنفسه، وكذا اللون والسواد لم يكن الله تعالى بثبوت القدرة له عليه موصوفا بكونه موجودا بذلك الوجود الراجع إلى ذات ذلك الموجود، ولا بكونه عرضا ولونا وسوادا بتلك المعاني الراجعة إلى ذوات تلك الأعراض، فلو كان الخلق هو عين المخلوق، لما كان الله تعالى به خالقا بل كان ما هو الخلق خالقا كما كان ما هو المكون لنفسه من الأعراض كاينا، وما هو البقاء لنفسه من صفات الباري باقيا على ما بينا في مسألة الصفات. فإذا على هذا كل ما وجد بعد العدم فهو الخالق لنفسه لا خالق له سواه ولا موجد، وهذا هو عين التعطيل والقول بنفي الصانع، والقول بذلك موجب القول بقدم العالم، إذ ما لا موجد له وكان وجوده بنفسه كان قديما على ما قررنا فإذا لم يكن هو خالقا بنفسه فغيره الذي ليس يخلق بنفسه ولا قام به الخلق أولى أن لا يكون خالقا.
From those who bring disbelief into existence, even though they are contradictory in that, abandoning this principle regarding the moving and the stationary and other than that as we have clarified. As for the Ash'aris, they helped us to deem the Mu'tazilites wrong in those issues and to attribute them to contradiction and departure from what is rational. Then here they come with something that necessitates the demolition of those principles and the correction of the doctrines of the opponents; this is an egregious contradiction. What verifies this is that any existent, since it is existent in itself, so also any accident, since it is an accident in itself, and similarly color and blackness—God the Exalted is not described, by virtue of His having power over them, as being existent with that existence that pertains to the essence of that existent, nor as being accident, color, or blackness with those meanings that pertain to the essences of those accidents. So if creation were the very same as the created, God the Exalted would not be a creator by it, rather that which is creation would be a creator, just as that which is self-constituting among accidents is self-constituting, and that which is self-subsisting among the attributes of the Creator is subsisting, as we have clarified in the issue of attributes. Thus, according to this, everything that comes into existence after non-existence is its own creator; there is no creator for it other than itself and no bringer into existence. This is pure atheism and the statement of negating the Creator, and saying that necessitates the statement of the eternity of the world, since that which has no bringer into existence and its existence is by itself would be eternal according to what we have established. So if it is not a creator by itself, then other than it, which does not create by itself and creation does not subsist in it, is even more deserving of not being a creator.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari position that creation (khalq) is identical to the created (makhluq). The author begins by noting the Ash'aris' inconsistency: they previously agreed with the Maturidis in criticizing the Mu'tazila for contradictions, but now they adopt a view that leads to greater contradictions. The core argument is that if creation is identical to the created, then the created thing would be the creator of itself, since creation is what brings it into existence. The author explains that an existent is what it is in itself, and an accident is what it is in itself; God is not described by the existence or accident status of things. So if creation were the same as the created, then the created would be its own creator. This leads to the conclusion that everything coming into existence after non-existence is its own creator, which is pure atheism (ta'til) and denial of the Creator. Moreover, this implies the eternity of the world, since anything existing by itself without a creator would be eternal. Therefore, the Ash'ari view cannot hold.

Correction: The author clarifies that God is not described by the existence or accidenthood of creatures; His power does not entail such identity.

B) Creation itself would be the creator.

Key Takeaway
If creation equals created, then the created thing would be its own creator, which contradicts the principle that things are self-referential in their existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If creation equaling created would make the created a creator, then what happens when you attribute your talents, wealth, or achievements to yourself rather than to the One who gave them? Will you repent and return every credit to Allah now?

From Creation-Identity to Atheism
Author derives atheism and eternal world from Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari position that creation is identical to the created logically leads to atheism (ta'til) and the belief in the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
If everything is self-created, then God is unnecessary and the world is eternal.

The author now draws the devastating conclusion from the Ash'ari premise. If creation equals the created, then every thing that comes into existence is its own creator. This is nothing less than pure atheism—the denial of a Creator. Moreover, it forces the conclusion that the world is eternal, since anything existing by itself without a cause is eternal. The author thus shows the Ash'ari view self-destructs.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the conclusion from the conditional
The author states: if creation = created, then everything that comes into existence is its own creator. Read the Arabic line 'فإذا على هذا كل ما وجد بعد العدم فهو الخالق لنفسه'.
This is the logical outcome.
2
Recognize the charge of atheism
The author calls this 'عين التعطيل' (pure atheism) and 'القول بنفي الصانع' (denial of the Creator). Understand that ta'til means stripping God of His attributes or denying Him altogether.
This is a severe accusation.
3
Connect to the eternity of the world
The author argues that if a thing has no creator and exists by itself, then it must be eternal (qadim). This follows from the principle that what exists without a cause is uncaused and therefore eternal.
This is a standard kalam argument.
4
See the final reductio
The author concludes: if the thing is not a creator by itself, then other things (like God) that neither create by themselves nor have creation subsist in them are even less deserving of being creators. This completes the refutation.
The Ash'ari position is self-defeating.

The Reductio Proof for a Specifier of Existence
Al-Nasafi argues for a specifier using a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses a reductio ad absurdum to prove that the world's existence requires a specifier distinct from its essence.

Core Idea
If the world could exist without a cause, then all change would be random and causality collapses.

This lesson examines a central rational proof in Maturidi theology: the necessity of a specifier (mukhassis) for the world's transition from non-existence to existence. Imam al-Nasafi argues that denying this leads to absurd consequences, thereby confirming the Islamic doctrine and refuting opponents like dualists and materialists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to see the flow. Then read the English translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'معدوم' (non-existent) and 'ممكن' (possible).
2
Identify the premise
Al-Nasafi starts with the premise that the world and each part were non-existent, and existence was possible. This is the starting point of the proof.
3
Understand the conditional argument
He argues that if existence could occur without a specifier, then any change (mixing, stillness, etc.) could occur without cause. This is the core of the reductio.
Ask yourself: Is this analogy valid? The commentary will explain.
4
See the absurd conclusion
The argument leads to the acceptance of dualist and materialist doctrines, which are false. This demonstrates that the original assumption is false.
The opponents here are those who deny a specifier.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider why this is a strong argument: it shows that rejecting a specifier leads to a complete breakdown of causality.
This is a typical kalam argument: necessitating a creator from the contingency of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق مذهبنا ويوجب بطلان مذهب (٥) الخصوم: أن العالم كان معدوما وكذا كان جزء من أجزائه على التعين، وكان يجوز عليه الوجود فكان العدم متحققا والوجود ممكنا فلو جاز اختصاصه بالوجود الممكن عن العدم المتحقق من غير تخصيص مخصص (۱) له بذلك وراء ذاته بل بما هو عين ذاته، لجاز اختصاص ما كان افتراقه متحققا بالامتزاج الممكن من غير معنى وراء ذاته بل هو عين ذاته. وكذا في كل مجتمع افترق أو مفترق اجتمع ، وكل متحرك سكن وكل ساكن متحرك وكذا في جميع الأعراض، فيؤدي إلى القول بصحة مذاهب الثنوية ونفاة الأعراض من الدهرية وغيرهم.
And that which confirms our doctrine and necessitates the invalidity of the opponents' doctrine: that the world was non-existent, and likewise each of its parts was [non-existent] in a determinate manner, and existence was possible for it. So non-existence was realized and existence was possible. If it were permissible for it to be specified with possible existence from realized non-existence without the specification of a specifier for it besides its essence, but rather by what is identical to its essence, then it would be permissible for that whose separation was realized to be specified with possible mixing without a meaning beyond its essence, but rather it is identical to its essence. And likewise in every compound that separated or separated that came together, and every moving that became still and every still that moved, and likewise in all accidents. This leads to the validity of the doctrines of the dualists and the deniers of accidents from the materialists and others.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (istidlal) for the necessity of a specifier (mukhassis) for the world's existence. Al-Nasafi begins by stating that this proof confirms the Maturidi doctrine and refutes the opponents. He premises that the world and each of its parts were non-existent (ma'dum) and that existence (wujud) was possible (mumkin) for them. Non-existence was actual, existence merely potential. If it were possible for the world to acquire existence from non-existence without a specifier distinct from its essence—i.e., by itself alone—then by analogy, anything could acquire its opposite state without a cause: a separated thing could mix, a moving thing could become still, etc. This leads to absurd consequences such as the doctrines of the dualists (thanawiyya) who believe in two eternal principles, and the materialists (dahriyya) who deny accidents (a'rad). The proof is a reductio ad absurdum showing that rejection of a specifier entails the collapse of causality.

Key Terms
مذهبنا
خصوم
عالم
معدوم
وجود
إمكان
تخصيص
مخصص
أعراض
ثنوية
دهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof assumes what it tries to prove, i.e., it presumes that existence requires a cause.
Correction: The proof does not assume a cause; it starts from the conceded fact of non-existence and possibility, and shows that rejecting a cause leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what would follow if the world could become existent without a specifier?
A) The world would be eternal.
B) All changes could occur without cause, leading to the acceptance of dualist and materialist doctrines.
C) The world would be self-sufficient.
D) Only God would cause existence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's reductio demonstrates that denying a specifier for the world's existence leads to the collapse of causality, thereby confirming the necessity of a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though your very existence and each breath depend moment-by-moment on a specific, willful Specifier, or do you secretly act as if you are self-sufficient and chance alone determines outcomes? What would change in your daily routine if you truly believed the reductio's conclusion?
?
Al-Nasafi's proof shows that denying a specifier would make any outcome possible, including moral good and evil becoming random. If you are honest, do you sometimes treat your moral choices as if they are isolated from God's specification? How can you re-anchor your actions in the conviction that every moment of obedience is a gift from the Specifier?
?
The proof forces you to choose between a rational universe grounded in a single Creator and an irrational universe where anything could happen. Which universe do you actually live in according to your fears and hopes? Are your anxieties rooted in the latter, and how can you realign your heart with the certitude of God's specification?

Refuting the Analogy from the Witnessed World
Nasafi refutes the opponent's analogy from the witnessed world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's critique of the opponent's analogy between divine and human action, and why the act of bringing into being (takwin) is eternal.

Core Idea
Divine takwin is eternal; human acts are transient.

Nasafi begins by addressing the opponent's claim that God's act of creation is inseparable from the created object, based on an analogy from human acts. He argues that this analogy fails because in the witnessed world, acts do not persist, whereas divine takwin is eternal. This lesson unpacks that refutation and clarifies the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening sentences
Start with Nasafi's response: 'The sentence: we accept and say it. But if you mean it in every case, it is not accepted...' Notice how he challenges the opponent's claim.
Pay attention to the term 'takwin' (bringing into being).
2
Identify the analogy being criticized
The opponent analogizes from the witnessed world (al-shahid) where the act and object are inseparable. Nasafi points out a crucial difference: in human acts, the act does not persist, but divine takwin persists eternally.
This is a key distinction in Maturidi theology.
3
Understand the consequence
Nasafi argues that if takwin did not persist until it attaches to the object, it would perish instantly. Therefore, it cannot be like human acts. See how this refutes the analogy.
This lays the groundwork for the decisive argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجملة فمسلم ونحن نقول به، وإن عنيت به في كل حال فهو غير مسلم وهو محل النزاع، فلم قلت ذلك؟ ثم إنك تعتبر ذلك بما تقرر في حسك ووهمك أن لا انفكاك في الشاهد بين الفعل والمفعول فتقيس عليه الغائب، وبينهما فرق على ما مر من وجوب بقاء التكوين إلى أن يتعلق بالمكون والفعل في الشاهد لا بقاء له، فلو لم يتعلق بالمفعول لفني من ساعته فلا يتصور تعلقه به البتة فلم يكن فعلا، وقد مر تقرير هذا الكلام قبل هذا، والذي يقطع شغب الخصوم بأسرهم أن يقال: هل لوجود العالم تعلق بذات الباري وبصفة من صفاته؟ فإن قالوا: لا أنكروا كونه صانعا للعالم. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: أقديم ذلك المعنى أم محدث؟ فإن قالوا: محدث. سئلوا عن تعلق حدوثه بالباري أو بصفة من صفاته، فإن جوزوا وجود حادث لا بمحدث يلزمهم مثله في كل العالم. فإن قالوا: هو قديم. قيل: هل أوجب قدمه قدم العالم؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أقروا بقدم العالم، والتزموا ما ألزموا خصومهم، وإن قالوا: لا. أبطلوا دليلهم.
The sentence: we accept and say it. But if you mean it in every case, it is not accepted, and it is the point of dispute. So why do you say that? Then you consider that based on what is established in your sense and imagination that there is no separation in the witnessed between the act and the object acted upon, so you analogize from it the absent. But there is a difference between them as previously mentioned regarding the necessity of the persistence of takwin (bringing into being) until it attaches to the thing brought into being, while the act in the witnessed has no persistence. So if it did not attach to the object, it would perish instantaneously, so its attachment to it cannot be conceived at all, so it would not be an act. This speech has been established before this. And what cuts off the clamor of the opponents entirely is to say: Does the existence of the world have a relationship to the essence of the Creator and to an attribute of His attributes? If they say: No, they deny that He is the Maker of the world. If they say: Yes, it is said: Is that meaning eternal or originated? If they say: Originated, they are asked about the attachment of its origination to the Creator or to an attribute of His attributes. If they allow the existence of an originated thing without an originator, they would be forced to allow the like of it in the entire world. If they say: It is eternal, it is said: Does its eternity necessitate the eternity of the world? If they say: Yes, then they have affirmed the eternity of the world and accepted what they imposed on their opponents. If they say: No, they have invalidated their proof.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's position on the relationship between God's act (takwin) and the object. Nasafi begins by stating that the opponent's claim is not accepted in all cases. He then points out that the opponent is basing his argument on an analogy from the witnessed world, where the act and the object are inseparable. However, there is a difference: in the witnessed world, the act (like making) does not persist, whereas in the divine case, takwin is eternal. Nasafi then presents a decisive argument that cuts off all objections: he asks whether the existence of the world has a relationship to God's essence or attribute. If the opponent says no, they deny God as Maker. If yes, then that relationship (the meaning) is either eternal or originated. If originated, they must explain its origination; if they claim it can be originated without an originator, that leads to absurdity. If they say it is eternal, then either the eternity of that relationship necessitates the eternity of the world (which they deny) or it does not, which invalidates their own proof that the world is originated. This logical dilemma refutes the opponent.

Key Terms
التكوين (takwin)
المكون (mukawwan)
الشاهد (al-shahid)
الغائب (al-gha'ib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's act of creation is temporal like human acts.
Correction: Nasafi shows that divine takwin is an eternal attribute, unlike temporal human acts.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the key difference between divine takwin and human acts?
A) Human acts are eternal, divine takwin is temporal.
B) Divine takwin persists until it attaches to the object, while human acts do not.
C) Both are temporal.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's analogy from human acts fails because divine takwin is an eternal attribute, not a transient act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about Allah, do you unconsciously project your own limitations onto Him? This passage shows how even a seemingly logical analogy can lead to error. What specific false analogy about Allah have you caught yourself making, and how will you replace it with the correct understanding from revelation?
?
Nasafi’s refutation demands careful distinction between Creator and creation. In your daily worship, do you maintain that distinction, or do you sometimes treat Allah’s actions as if they were human? What concrete step will you take today to deepen your reverence and submission to His unique attributes?
?
This lesson exposes how a subtle error in reasoning can undermine aqeedah. Is there an area of your faith where you rely on your own reasoning or analogies instead of submitting to what Allah has said? Will you commit to studying the sources more carefully to avoid such mistakes? If so, what will be your first step?

The Decisive Logical Argument on the World's Relation to God
Nasafi presents a logical dilemma cutting off all objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's decisive argument that forces the opponent into a logical dilemma regarding the world's relation to God's essence or attribute.

Core Idea
The world's relation to God is either eternal or originated; both options refute the opponent.

After refuting the analogy from the witnessed world, Nasafi presents a powerful argument that cuts off all objections. He asks whether the world's existence has a relationship to God's essence or attribute, and then poses a dilemma about whether that relationship is eternal or originated. This lesson walks through the logical steps that decisively refute the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the decisive argument passage
Start from 'And what cuts off the clamor of the opponents entirely...' Follow the series of questions Nasafi poses.
This is a logical chain, so trace each step.
2
Trace the first question
Nasafi asks: Does the existence of the world have a relationship to God's essence or attribute? If no, they deny God as Maker. If yes, proceed.
This sets up the dilemma.
3
Analyze the two branches
If the relationship is originated, they must explain its origination; if they allow an originated thing without an originator, absurdity follows. If the relationship is eternal, ask whether its eternity necessitates the world's eternity. Either way, the opponent is refuted.
Notice how each branch leads to a conclusion that contradicts the opponent's position.

God's Eternal Attribute of Creation
Nasafi affirms God's eternal attribute of creation while denying the world's eternality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming God's eternal attribute of creation does not imply the world is eternal.

Core Idea
God's attribute of creation is eternal, but its ascription to the world is temporal.

This lesson tackles a subtle theological point: how can we say God is eternally Creator without implying the world is eternal? The author, Imam Nasafi, explains using the distinction between the attribute itself and its attachment (taʿalluq) to the created thing. Understanding this distinction is key to avoiding a common misconception.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the rhythm and key terms.
The passage ends abruptly but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two claims: (1) God is eternally Creator because the attribute of creation subsists in Him. (2) The world is not eternally created. Find these in the text.
Look for the phrases 'نقول' and 'ولا نقول'.
3
Reflect on the analogy with will
The author draws an analogy with God's will (irāda). Consider how the same distinction applies: God is eternally willing, but the object of will (the world's existence) is not eternal.
This analogy reinforces the point about ascription depending on attachment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نقول إنه تعالى كان في الأزل خالقا لقيام صفة الخلق، كما نقول : كان عالما قادرا مريدا . ولا نقول أيضا كان العالم في الأزل مخلوقا، لأن المعدوم لا يكون مخلوقا، لأنه إنما يصير مخلوقا بقبوله أثر التخليق، فقبل تعلق الخلق به لا يكون مخلوقا، وما لم يصر مخلوقا لا يضاف الخالق إليه، فلا يقال كان خالقا للعالم لأن الإضافة تثبت بتعليق الخلق بالمخلوق، فكانت الإضافة من مقتضيات التعلق لا من مقتضيات وجود صفة الخلق، واعتبر هذا بالإرادة فإن الله تعالى كان في الأزل مريدا ولا يقال: كان في الأزل مريدا وجود العالم ووجود العالم مراده في الأزل لما مر من الفرق، أو يمتنع عن الإضافة مخافة ثبوت وهم قدم ما دخل
Then we say: Indeed He existed in pre-eternity as Creator due to the existence of the attribute of creation, just as we say: He was knowing, powerful, willing. And we also do not say that the world was created in pre-eternity, because a non-existent cannot be created, for it only becomes created by accepting the effect of creation. So before the attachment of creation to it, it is not created. And as long as it has not become created, the Creator is not ascribed to it. So it is not said: He was Creator of the world, because the ascription is established by the attachment of creation to the created. So the ascription is from the requirements of the attachment, not from the requirements of the existence of the attribute of creation. And consider this with will: Indeed Allah existed in pre-eternity as willing, and it is not said: He was in pre-eternity willing the existence of the world, and the existence of the world was His will in pre-eternity, due to the distinction that passed, or he refrains from the ascription for fear of establishing the illusion of the pre-eternity of what entered
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage articulates the Māturīdī position on God's attribute of creation (khalq). It states that God is eternally (azalī) Creator because the attribute of creation subsists in Him from eternity, just as He is eternally knowing, powerful, and willing. However, this does not imply that the world is eternal. The reason is that a non-existent thing cannot be described as 'created' because creation is the effect of the creative act on an entity; before that act attaches, the thing remains non-existent and uncreated. The ascription 'Creator of the world' depends on the actual attachment of creation to the world, not merely on the existence of the attribute of creation in God. Thus, the ascription is a consequence of the temporal relation (taʿalluq), not of the eternal attribute itself. The author draws an analogy with will (irāda): God is eternally willing, but it is not said that He is eternally willing the existence of the world, because willing an object is distinct from the eternal attribute of will. The text ends abruptly, but the point is to avoid implying the pre-eternity of the world when affirming God's eternal creative attribute.

Key Terms
الأزل
خالق
صفة الخلق
مخلوق
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God is eternally Creator, then the world must be eternal.
Correction: The attribute of creation is eternal, but its ascription to the world is temporal because the world did not exist in pre-eternity. The world only becomes created when the creative act attaches to it.

Checkpoint
Why is it not said that God was Creator of the world in pre-eternity?
A) Because the attribute of creation does not exist in pre-eternity.
B) Because the world was not yet created to receive the ascription.
C) Because God's creation is only an action, not an attribute.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
God's attribute of creation is eternal, but the ascription 'Creator of the world' is temporal because it depends on the actual creation of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The analogy of will: God is eternally willing, but not eternally willing the existence of the world. How does this help you trust that God's will is completely independent of anything external? The ascription 'Creator of the world' only applies when the world actually exists.
